## Chapter 8 – The Museum of Lost Youth

The day began, as all of Aurelio’s days did, with an overelaborate ritual that felt more like a ceremony than a morning routine. He had the habit of lighting a single candle, the one with the pale blue wax that caught the light like a small star, and then pouring his coffee with a flourish that made the drink itself seem to applaud. But today he abandoned the ritual. The candle lay untouched in the corner of his study, its flame flickering once, then dying, as if it had been promised to be snuffed long before the sun rose.

He dressed as he always did—slim, tailored, with a silk collar, but without the extra preening that usually marked the preparation of his public self. He walked to the windows, where the light of the morning was not yet sharp enough to cut the shadows from the old oak floors. The city was still a hushed, gray whisper, the kind of morning that made him think of his own breath, heavy and deliberate. He felt, as if he could hear it, the faint pulse of the city, like a heartbeat that was not his own.

The purpose of his day was simple: to visit Professor Ferrante. Aurelio had known the professor from a much earlier time, when they were both young intellectuals, each a budding star in their own right. Ferrante was the one who had first named Aurelio’s essays “the purest form of shining stone,” and, perhaps more importantly, the one who had first drawn him into the world of critical analysis and aesthetics. Now, the professor was a frail man, with a crumbling facade that looked as if it had once been marble but was now bleached and cracked.

Aurelio’s heart beat with a strange mixture of anticipation and dread. He had been so careful to keep his life as a museum, a curation of flawless pieces, that the idea of confronting a piece that had already degraded felt like a breach of the sanctuary he had built. He imagined, in the back of his mind, Ferrante’s once-sharp eyes, his lines of text, his once-boisterous laughter, all now reduced to a mere echo.

The nursing home was a building that sat on the edge of an old Milan suburb, a place of quiet that had always seemed far too far from the glittering world his life had become. The garden that surrounded it was a testament to careful neglect—flowers that had once been the subject of his most vibrant essays, now wilting as if to remind him that even the most beautiful things succumb to time. He walked up the path, his gaze following the same stones he had once described in a long, celebrated essay on the fleeting nature of architecture.

Inside, the corridors smelled of antiseptic and tired hope. He was welcomed by a young nurse with a name tag that read "Lara." She was the keeper of the day, the one who took the elevator, the one who whispered a quiet “Buongiorno, signor Vianni” with the gentility of a librarian. He followed her down into the common room, where several elderly people sat, watching the television or clutching the armrests of their tables with a steadiness that betrayed a lifetime of anticipation.

The first thing that struck him was the silence that had settled over the room; a silence that was almost palpable, as if the walls were holding their breath. He found his way to the corner where Professor Ferrante was sitting, his eyes closed, a small smile playing on his lips, as if he were remembering a joyous memory. The old man had a white beard, the kind that was as unruly as the way he used to argue with his students about the nuances of modernist painting. His hair had all but vanished, and the skin on his cheek was the paper-thin, translucent memory of a woman’s soul, and yet his eyes glimmered with the same intensity he had shown in his early decades.

Aurelio approached him with a hesitation that startled him. He did not want to be seen as a voyeur, but he could not deny that a part of him was craving the confrontation. His throat tightened as he opened his mouth to speak.

"Signor Ferrante," he began, his voice dropping to a tone that was more reverent than he had ever used in front of anyone. "I…I have come because I thought…"

The professor's eyes flicked open, a soft, knowing glint that made Aurelio's heart pound. "Vianni, my boy," he said, his voice low and rough as gravel. "You always returned to my rooms like a pilgrimage."

Aurelio's mind flickered to the days when they had been together, arguing about the penetration of the human spirit in art. He remembered the way the professor would laugh, a booming sound that seemed to spill out of the walls, and feel the way the world was not only beautiful but also cruelly indifferent. He thought of the times Aurelio had sat in the back of the lecture hall, his pen scratching like a small rebellion.

"You have grown," Ferrante said, gesturing to an empty chair across from him. "And so have I, in a different dimension."

Aurelio took the chair, his hands trembling as they sank into the armrests, the memory of the weight of his own skin on his fingers as if he were looking at a mirror but with a new, more distant piece of glass. He looked at Ferrante again—his eyes, now a little younger, but lined with the kind of experience that could not be replicated in any other life. The face of the man he had idolized in his youth was now dressed in the same clothes of consequence that his own face wore: sorrow, patience, and an indulgence that came from a deep and honest love of beauty.

The professor turned his head slowly, as if to scan the room for a hidden observer. He began speaking with a voice that sounded almost like a prayer.

"You made me think," he said, as he spoke. "You made the world a place of lights, a place of dangerous beauty, and you taught me that those lights can become a curse, a threat to all of us in our own way. And now…now it is for me to look at you, and to see what I had once promised to be—to be the only one who sees beauty in your eyes, and yet I am no longer interested…"

Aurelio was lost. He had always thought that he had seen beauty in Ferrante’s eyes, but now, to him, it felt like an old illusion he had forgotten to remember. He looked at the old man in front of him, feeling a sudden sense that his own life had become a museum of lost youth.

Ferrante smiled, a small, almost sarcastic smile that cut through it all. "I had always known that the thing we try to hold onto is the thing that is slipping away," he said, as if he had read the sentence in a book that Aurelio had written. "And yet we are still at the same time that we have stuck to the idea."

The conversation turned to a topic that seemed almost inevitable, an all-encompassing theme that was both a necessary moment and a very painful one: the concept of mortality. Ferrante leaned forward and spoke about the inevitability of time, the hollowness of the young man who was determined to stay alive, and the loss of meaning that reality forces at times. He voiced the idea that there was no way to avoid death, no way to hold on to beauty. He made Aurelio feel as if he were a part of a conversation that his mind had never intended to be part of. He had always assumed that art was a way to escape death, but now he saw it as a mechanism that our children use to realize that the world is brittle and fragile.

When the conversation ended, Aurelio left the nursing home in a daze. He had been pushed by an indescribable sense of sadness, which was almost a sorcery that had happened to him in a sudden and bizarre way. He had never imagined that his muse could be a nurse or a scientist. He had thought that it was only the possibility that he had to remember that he was still very young, that his life was still a new beginning, that the world was still a place where he could continue to grow. But it was now, after his conversation with Ferrante, that he understood that he had to accept the fact that his life was an ending, that he had to accept himself and his own reality, and that he had to find a way to live, even if it was with a different perspective.

He was no longer able to see his life as a museum. He saw the world as he would see a museum that was in the process of deteriorating. He had to accept the reality that the world was in a state of flux, that there was no way to get rid of the fact that time would not take his beauty. He had learned that the only way to continue to live was to accept the fact that his life was not a museum, that his life was something else. He had to accept the fact that he can no longer live, and that he must look for his own path, even if it was a different path.

Aurelio walked home with the idea that he had to find a new life, perhaps a new meaning, because it was a very different sense of life. He had to realize that the world had a new time, that he had to accept this new reality.

---

## Chapter 9 — The Pharmacist's Cabinet

The first vial arrived on a Tuesday, in a box the size of a rosary case, wrapped in black tissue that smelled of nothing—not perfume, not the antique shop mustiness Aurelio had expected, but the clinical absence of scent. He held it under the kitchen light like a jeweler examining a flawed stone and read the label twice: *HGH – Sottocontrollo, uso estetico sperimentale*. Human growth hormone. Experimental cosmetic use.

His hands did not tremble. That was the first wrong thing. He had expected his hands to tremble, or at least to betray some flicker of the guilt he had been promised would arrive hand-in-hand with vanity. But there was only curiosity—a bright, clean curiosity, the same one that had made him fall in love with a single paragraph of Proust at fifteen, the one about the madeleine dissolving on the tongue. He uncapped it. Drew the syringe. Injected himself in the hip, the way Dr. Marenghi had demonstrated, with the practiced ease of a man who had done this many times before—not to himself, not yet, but to others. Others who were paying.

Dr. Marenghi's office was not an office. It was a narrow room behind a pharmacy in the Isola district, between a shuttered wine bar and a shop selling nativity figurines, and it smelled of rubbing alcohol and bergamot. The doctor himself was a slim man in his forties with hands like a pianist's—long-fingered, precise, unsettlingly calm—and he never sat down during their consultations. He moved around Aurelio the way a portrait painter circles the model, cataloguing imperfections with an eye that was simultaneously clinical and aesthetic.

"You see this," Marenghi said, pointing to the faint line near Aurelio's left eye, the one that had appeared three weeks ago and that Aurelio now checked in every reflective surface he passed—the darkened window of a café, the polished steel of an elevator door, the screen of his own phone, which he would unlock only to stare at his own face as though it belonged to someone he was trying to remember. "This is the first sentence of a longer text. And once that text begins, it does not stop."

"What text?"

"The text of what you are not willing to become."

Aurelio laughed. It was a good laugh—controlled, warm, the kind that made waiters bring another glass of wine without being asked. But something behind his sternum went cold, a small door closing in a room he didn't know he had.

He began coming every two weeks. Then every week. Then twice a week.

The regimen accumulated. Peptides he injected into the hollows of his temples. A topical retinoid that made his skin peel for days, raw and translucent as onion skin, before revealing something beneath that was almost—*almost*—what it had been. A course of laser treatments that left his face feeling sunburned and strangely euphoric, as though his skin had been forcibly reminded of its own vitality. Injectable fillers along the nasolabial folds that Marenghi administered with a needle so fine it barely left a pinprick, and for a few days Aurelio would look in the mirror and see himself again—not the wasted version, not the one with the crepe-thin eyelids and the jaw that had begun to soften—but the original. The masterpiece. The twenty-six-year-old who had made a room fall silent simply by entering it.

He knew what he was doing. He was not stupid. He was, in fact, the kind of intelligent that made his stupidity more terrifying, because it wore the mask of reason. He told himself the treatments were maintenance, the way one maintained a painting or a body of work. He told himself he was simply refusing to let talent curdle. He told himself, with the serene dishonesty of a man who has begun lying to himself in earnest, that this was not vanity. This was *rigor*.

Dr. Marenghi, for his part, said nothing that could be construed as a warning. He nodded. He adjusted dosages. He kept meticulous records in a leather-bound notebook. And when Aurelio mentioned, one evening, that the fillers seemed to be lasting less time than before—that his face was metabolizing the work faster, swallowing it, *hungry* for it—the doctor simply tilted his head and said, "Good. It means your body is alive. We want it alive."

The cost was not a problem. Not yet. It was, in fact, barely visible—a line item buried in his account statements between the restaurant at Via della Spiga and the subscription to the literary quarterly he had stopped reading but hadn't canceled. He told himself the spending was proportional to the return. Youth was the only currency that compounded. Beauty invested in beauty. The interest was inevitable.

But there were smaller costs that Marenghi didn't itemize. The supplements that arrived in plain envelopes—NMN capsules, alpha-lipoic acid, a powdered collagen from deep-sea fish that tasted like chalk and regret. The cryotherapy sessions, twenty minutes in a chamber so cold it made his eyes water and his thoughts stop, and for those twenty minutes he was nothing—no wit, no elegance, no curated self—just a body shivering in a white room, and the silence of that was so total it frightened him more than anything else.

He began canceling dinners. Not all at once. First Luca. Then the evening with Giulia and her husband, which he rescheduled three times until Giulia sent a single message—*Where are you?*—and he didn't answer. Then Dario, who called once, twice, a third time, and on the fourth Aurelio picked up and said, "I'm fine," in a voice so carefully modulated that Dario hung up without saying goodbye.

He told himself he was conserving energy. That beauty required solitude the way a flame requires air—intermittently, and on its own terms. He told himself the solitude was deliberate, architectural, a choice. But the truth was simpler and uglier: he couldn't bear to be seen while he was in the middle of being undone.

Because that was the thing he wouldn't say, even to himself. The treatments weren't working. Or rather—they were working in the way a fever works. They produced heat, flushed the skin, made the eyes look bright for a few days, and then the body settled back into whatever temperature it had decided was its own. The line near his eye was still there. His jaw still softened in the late afternoon. His hair, which he now photographed every morning from four angles, was beginning to thin at the temples in a way that no supplement or serum could reverse, because it was not a cosmetic problem. It was a *temporal* one. It was time arriving, with luggage, at his door.

He found himself standing in the pharmacy at midnight, alone, holding a jar of testosterone cream that Marenghi had recommended with the casualness of a man recommending a good wine. The pharmacist—a woman with reading glasses on a chain—looked at him and, for one unguarded moment, looked *through* him. Not at the face. At the man behind it. Aurelio felt that look like a needle between the ribs, and he paid without counting the change and walked home through streets that were empty and beautiful and indifferent to him, and in the dark of his bedroom he stood before the mirror and saw, for the first time, not a man losing his face, but a man losing his grip on the story he had told himself about who he was.

He pressed his palms against the glass. The glass did not press back.

*How much longer*, he thought, and the question was not about the treatments, or the supplements, or the fillers. It was about the whole elaborate architecture of *him*—the salon, the prose, the face, the name that opened doors—and whether any of it was load-bearing, or whether it had always been scaffolding, and he was now standing in an empty room pretending the walls were still there.

Dr. Marenghi would have a protocol for this. Marenghi always had a protocol.

Aurelio picked up the phone.

## Chapter 10: The Erosion of the Circle

The silence of the palazzo had become a physical presence, a heavy, velvet curtain that muffled the sounds of the Milanese streets outside. For years, this house had been a sanctuary of noise—the rhythmic clinking of crystal, the melodic overlap of three different conversations, the laughter that acted as a soundtrack to Aurelio’s perceived divinity. Now, the echoes felt mocking.

Aurelio stood before the triptych of mirrors in his dressing room, the lighting calibrated to a surgical precision that left no shadow unexamined. He was not looking at himself so much as he was auditing a failing asset. He leaned in, his breath fogging the glass, tracing the perimeter of his jawline. Was it sagging? Or was it merely the light? He spent forty minutes applying a serum that cost more than a laborer’s monthly wage, massaging it into his skin with a desperation that bordered on the violent. 

*It is a war,* he told himself, his inner voice a frantic whisper. *A war of attrition. Every second is a skirmish, every hour a lost territory. I cannot surrender a single millimeter of my face to the gravity of the common man.*

The doorbell rang, a sharp, intrusive sound that made him flinch. He didn't move for several minutes, paralyzed by the sudden anxiety of being perceived. To be seen was to be judged; to be judged was to be found wanting. 

When he finally opened the door, Luca stood there. Luca, who had been his shadow since their university days, whose loyalty was as steady as a heartbeat. He was carrying a bottle of vintage Barolo and wearing a look of profound, quiet concern that Aurelio found utterly repulsive.

"You’ve missed the last three Thursdays, Aurelio," Luca said, stepping inside without waiting for an invitation. He looked around the foyer, noting the dimmed lights and the thin layer of dust on the console table. "The group is starting to ask. Not out of curiosity, but out of worry. You’ve become a ghost in your own house."

Aurelio closed the door with a click that sounded like a gavel. He didn't embrace Luca; he stepped back, instinctively creating a distance that allowed him to scan Luca’s face. He saw it immediately—the deepening of the nasolabial folds, the slight thinning of the hair at the temples. Luca was aging. It was a slow, inevitable collapse, and seeing it in his best friend felt like watching a mirror crack in real-time.

"I’ve been working," Aurelio lied, his voice smooth but brittle. "The new series of essays requires a certain... monastic isolation. I cannot be the center of a salon when my mind is occupied by the architecture of the Baroque."

Luca sighed, a sound of exhaustion that grated on Aurelio’s nerves. "You aren't working, Aurelio. You’re hiding. I saw you last week at the gallery opening—or rather, I saw you for five minutes before you vanished into the restroom to stare at yourself in the mirror. You looked... frantic. Not the usual vanity, but something else. Something hungry."

Aurelio’s expression hardened. The word *hungry* felt too accurate. He was starving for a version of himself that no longer existed, or perhaps one that had only ever existed in his imagination.

"You imagine things, Luca. Your own nostalgia is coloring your perception," Aurelio snapped, turning away to pour two glasses of water, avoiding the wine. He had read in a dubious medical journal that tannins accelerated oxidative stress. 

"Stop it," Luca said, his voice rising. "Stop this performance. We are your friends. We love you—not the image you curate, not the 'God of the Room,' but *you*. But you’ve started treating us like witnesses to a crime. Every time we speak, you look at us as if we are reminders of a disease you’re trying to cure."

Aurelio froze, the glass halfway to his lips. The accusation hit the center of his obsession. He did see them that way. He saw his circle of friends not as companions, but as a countdown clock. Every wrinkle on Luca’s forehead was a warning; every grey hair on Dario’s head was a prophecy. To love them was to accept the fate they shared, and Aurelio found that prospect intolerable.

"Perhaps," Aurelio whispered, his voice trembling with a sudden, cold rage, "I simply have higher standards for my surroundings than you do. I refuse to slide into a comfortable decay. I refuse to be 'distinguished.' Distinguished is just a polite word for old."

"And what is the alternative?" Luca asked softly. "To spend your life in a pharmacy? To treat your skin like a canvas to be bleached? You’re erasing yourself, Aurelio. If you scrub away everything that shows time has passed, there will be nothing left of you but a blank, porcelain mask."

Aurelio lashed out. It wasn't a physical blow, but a verbal one, delivered with the surgical precision of the critic he was. "And look at you, Luca. You’ve accepted it. You’ve surrendered. You wear your mediocrity like a badge of honor, pretending that 'wisdom' compensates for the loss of beauty. It doesn't. In this world, the only wisdom that matters is the power to remain desired."

The silence that followed was different from the silence of the house. It was a vacuum, sucking the air out of the room. Luca looked at him—not with anger, but with a devastating kind of pity.

"I used to admire your ambition," Luca said, setting the wine bottle on the table. "But this isn't ambition. It's a pathology. You’re so afraid of the sunset that you’re closing the curtains on everyone who actually cares about you."

Luca left without another word. The sound of the door closing was final, a punctuation mark at the end of a long, decaying sentence.

Aurelio stood alone in the center of the room. He felt a surge of triumph—he had defended his position, he had remained superior—but it was immediately followed by a hollow, aching void. He walked back to the mirror.

He began to analyze the skin around his eyes again. He imagined the cells dying, the collagen snapping like old rubber bands. He thought of Dr. Marenghi’s promise: *a new protocol, a deeper infusion, a way to freeze the clock.*

He realized then that the salon gatherings had stopped because he had stopped summoning them. He had become the architect of his own isolation. He had traded the warmth of human connection for the cold sterility of a preserved image. 

*They don't understand,* he told himself, leaning closer until his nose touched the glass. *They want me to fall with them. They want the comfort of shared ruin.*

But as he stared into his own pupils, he saw a flicker of something that terrified him. It wasn't a wrinkle or a spot. It was a look of profound loneliness. For a fleeting second, the mask slipped, and he saw a man who was not a god, but a prisoner—a prisoner in a palace of mirrors, guarded by the ghost of his own youth.

He quickly blinked the emotion away. He reached for the phone to call Dr. Marenghi. He needed a stronger dose. He needed something that would numb the fear and sharpen the facade. 

As he dialed, he caught a glimpse of the portrait Dario was painting in the other room—the one he had criticized so harshly. In the dim light, the painting looked honest. It looked human. With a sudden, violent impulse, Aurelio threw a silk cloth over the canvas, hiding it from view.

He could not bear to see the truth. He preferred the beautiful lie, even if he had to inhabit it alone.

The erosion of the circle was complete. The satellites had drifted away, leaving the star to burn in a vacuum, blindingly bright and utterly cold. He lay down on his bed, the expensive linens feeling like a shroud, and waited for the chemicals to take hold, dreaming of a world where time was merely a suggestion and beauty was the only law.

---

# Chapter 11 — The Woman in the Mirror's Edge

The morning mist clung to the marble floors of Aurelio’s palazzo like a lover, sighing softly as the sun crept in through the high windows. Aurelio stirred beneath his weighted quilt, his mind already darting through the day’s demands: the stylized speeches, the meticulous sketches, the whispered confessions in the bustling corridors. But today, his gaze was fixed not on the velvet drapes or the polished mirrors, but on the reflection that stared back—faint, but unmistakable.

A line. Just a whisper. Beneath the lids of his sleep, it pulsed like a secret caught in his fingertips. Aurelio’s breath caught, his hand tightening around the edge of the bed. The world seemed to hold its breath. He blinked, the light shifting, the reflection softening, until the line vanished. But it lingered, a ghost in the glass, insistent and unforgettable.

He rose, slow and deliberate, the silver drab of his cuffs catching the light. Each step was measured, a ritual he took to remind himself of his own reign—his voice, his will, his brilliance. But now, the mirror told a different story. Some things, he realized, could not be erased.

Aurelio moved through the salon with a new kind of caution, his presence less magnetic, less unassailable. Yet, he could not bring himself to leave. The friends, the admirers, even the friends who had once idolized him—now their eyes held a different weight, a promise of change. And in that shifting, Aurelio felt the old ache of imperfection creeping in, like a chill at the edge of a forgotten room.

He adjusted his collar, the fabric whispering against his skin, and stepped into the room. The air was thick with the scent of old leather and unspoken secrets. His friends gathered around, their eyes lingering at him, as if waiting for him to prove again that he was more than just a man caught between time and hubris.

One voice broke the tension. “Aurelio, you look… different.”

He turned, the reflection catching the light, but something had shifted. Not in the way he’d wished, but in the way a mirror can show what lies beneath a mask. His voice, when it came, was firmer, less polished, more raw—like a man confronting a mirror for the first time.

The conversation was brittle. It unfurled like a fragile thread, revealing layers of doubt, fear, and the unspoken truth that beneath the surface, Aurelio was not the god of the room anymore. Yet, somewhere in that shifting, he felt the flicker of something else: hope. Not the kind that promised forever, but the kind that whispered, *“Maybe this is enough.”*

As the day waned, Aurelio found himself standing alone at the edge of the villa, the letters of his friends tucked into his pocket. He stared at the horizon, the city lights blurring into a slow, golden haze. The line had returned, but this time, it was not just a trace of beauty—it was a reminder of the quiet war he fought within, a battle between pride and vulnerability.

The world outside moved with its own rhythms, indifferent to the tremors within. But in that moment, Aurelio knew: he had to choose. Would he continue chasing the illusion of immortality, or would he embrace the truth that even in decay, there was still a kind of light?

---

Aurelio returned to his study that evening, the scent of ink and old paper wrapping around him like a familiar embrace. He sat at his desk, the soft hum of the city outside a distant murmur, and opened a journal. The pages were stained with ink, each entry a confession, a confession, a reckoning. He wrote in a voice that still carried the weight of his celebrity, but now it trembled with vulnerability.

*“Today, I saw a flicker behind the glass. A crack in the mirror. It was not a sign of defeat, but of change. I am not the man I once was. And perhaps, that is the greatest beauty.”*

He closed the journal, the words settling heavier than the silence in the room. The line he had once feared now felt less like a curse and more like a threshold—a portal to something deeper, something he had never truly understood until now.

In the quiet after, Aurelio allowed himself a small, trembling smile. The world might be changing, and so would he. But for the first time, he allowed himself to believe that change, too, could be grace.

---

## Chapter 12: The Right Dose of Madness

The mirror had become a courtroom, and Aurelio Vianni was both the defendant and the hanging judge. 

For weeks, he had scrutinized his reflection under the clinical, unforgiving glare of the bathroom LEDs, searching for the betrayal. He saw it now—not as a single line, but as a subtle shift in the topography of his face. A microscopic sagging of the jawline, a shadow beneath the eyes that no amount of Dr. Marenghi’s expensive creams could erase. To the world, he was still a paragon of grace; to himself, he was a crumbling monument, a fresco peeling away in the dampness of a forgotten chapel.

The terror was no longer a seed; it was a forest, thick and suffocating. 

*I cannot be a relic,* he whispered to the glass. *I refuse to be a curated memory.*

The transition began not with a crash, but with a chemical hum. It started with the stimulants—small, white pills provided by a contact in the underground art scene, designed to sharpen the mind and erase the need for sleep. At first, they were merely tools for productivity. But soon, the productivity became an end in itself. Aurelio discovered that when the brain is overclocked, the body becomes a secondary concern. When he was vibrating at a frequency that made the air seem to shimmer, he forgot the lines. He forgot the clock. He felt not just young, but timeless—a creature of pure electricity.

He stopped sleeping. Sleep was the interval where the mind reconciled with the body, and Aurelio no longer wished to negotiate with his flesh. He spent seventy-two hours awake, the walls of his palazzo beginning to breathe in rhythm with his racing heart. He wandered the streets of Milan at three in the morning, the city a charcoal sketch of silence and shadow. He felt like a ghost haunting his own life, a phantom of elegance gliding through the Navigli, his eyes dilated and glittering with a manic, artificial light.

In these nocturnal excursions, he sought sensation as a form of verification. He would touch the cold stone of the Duomo, press his palms against the freezing metal of a closed shutter, or stand in the middle of a rainstorm without an umbrella, laughing as the water soaked through his cashmere coat. He needed to feel the friction of existence. The numbness of his perfection had been a prison; this new, jagged edge of instability felt like liberation.

*This is the secret,* he told himself, his thoughts racing like stallions in a storm. *The others—Luca, Dario—they accept the slow slide into the grave. They call it 'maturity.' But maturity is just a polite word for surrender. I will not surrender. I will burn so brightly that the darkness cannot find me.*

He returned to his study, the room illuminated only by a single desk lamp and the blue glow of his laptop. The silence of the house was heavy, but inside his head, there was a symphony. He began to write.

For years, Aurelio’s prose had been a polished mirror—elegant, precise, admired for its symmetry. Now, the mirror shattered. He began to produce essays that were feverish, torrential, and dangerously unhinged. He wrote about the "Erotics of Decay," the "Theology of the Void," and the "Tyranny of the Eternal Now." He discarded the formal structures of art criticism, replacing them with a prose that read like a manifesto written in blood and gold. 

He wrote for twelve hours straight, his pen scratching violently against the paper, his mind leaping from one association to another with a velocity that would have terrified him months ago. He described beauty not as a state of being, but as a violent act of defiance against the universe. He argued that the only true art was that which acknowledged its own inevitable destruction.

*The right dose of madness,* he mused, pausing to stare at his trembling hand, *is the only cure for the boredom of perfection.*

He began publishing these pieces anonymously on a digital forum, a sanctuary for the disillusioned and the avant-garde. To his surprise, they did not meet with confusion, but with adoration. A new audience emerged—younger, hungrier, more volatile than the salon crowd he had once curated. These were children of the digital age, souls who recognized the scent of a breakdown and mistook it for genius. They called him a visionary. They called his work "visceral."

For the first time in his life, Aurelio felt a thrill that had nothing to do with being admired for his face. He was being admired for his chaos.

Yet, the chemicals demanded a higher price. The stimulants had stripped away his filters, leaving his nerves raw and exposed. He became irritable, his mood swinging from ecstatic heights to abyssal lows in the span of a heartbeat. He stopped answering Luca’s calls. He ignored the concerned messages from his publisher. When he did see his old friends, he looked at them with a mixture of pity and disgust. They seemed so slow, so heavy, so tethered to the earth by the weight of their own aging.

One evening, while idling in a café with Dario, Aurelio found himself staring at the painter’s hand. He noticed the slight tremor in Dario’s fingers, the way the skin had grown translucent, revealing the blue veins beneath. 

"You're staring, Aurelio," Dario said, his voice weary.

"You're fading, Dario," Aurelio replied, his voice thin and vibrating. "Can't you feel it? The edges are blurring. You're becoming a sketch of yourself."

Dario looked at him, and for the first time, there was no warmth in the painter's eyes—only a profound, echoing sadness. "And you, my friend, are becoming a scream. You look like you've been hollowed out. There is a light in your eyes, yes, but it's the light of a house on fire."

Aurelio laughed, a sharp, brittle sound that made the people at the next table turn and stare. "Then let it burn! I would rather be a blaze for a moment than a candle for a century. Don't you see? This is the only way to stay alive. To push the sensation until it breaks you. To live at the very limit of the nerves."

"This isn't living," Dario whispered. "This is a performance of life."

Aurelio dismissed him with a wave of his hand, his mind already drifting back to the rhythmic pulse of his thoughts. He didn't care if it was a performance. If the mask was the only thing that remained, he would make the mask a masterpiece of madness.

As the days blurred into a singular, shimmering haze, Aurelio found himself thinking of Celeste. 

She was the only one who didn't look at him with pity or confusion. When they had met, she had spoken of the beauty of cracks, of the way a painting’s craquelure told the story of its survival. He remembered the way she looked at him—not as a god, but as a curiosity. She saw the fractures he was trying so desperately to hide, and instead of recoiling, she seemed to lean in, as if trying to hear the music coming from the breaks.

*She understands,* he thought, pacing the length of his bedroom at four in the morning. *She knows that the perfection I chased was a lie. The only truth is the erosion.*

He felt a sudden, desperate urge to see her, to tell her about the white pills and the sleepless nights, to show her the feverish manuscripts scattered across his floor like fallen leaves. He wanted to be seen in his ugliness, provided that ugliness was framed as a radical choice. He wanted her to validate his descent, to tell him that this madness was not a collapse, but an ascension.

But as the sun began to bleed over the Milanese skyline, painting the city in shades of bruised purple and gold, Aurelio caught sight of himself in the mirror once more. 

He was thinner. His cheekbones were more pronounced, almost skeletal. There were dark circles under his eyes that looked like charcoal smudges. But he didn't feel the dread he had felt in Chapter Five. Instead, he felt a surge of pride. He looked like a martyr of his own making. He looked like a man who had traded his peace for a glimpse of the infinite.

He picked up his pen and wrote a single sentence at the top of a new page: *The only way to defeat time is to outrun it in the direction of insanity.*

He smiled, and for the first time, the smile didn't feel curated. It felt dangerous. He was no longer the god of the room; he was the ghost in the machine, the glitch in the gallery. He felt an intoxicating sense of power, a belief that he had finally found the key to a happiness that didn't depend on the approval of a mirror.

He was selfish, he knew. He had alienated his friends, burned his bridges, and poisoned his blood. But as he looked out over the waking city, Aurelio Vianni felt an absolute, shimmering certainty: he was finally, truly, awake.

He didn't realize that he was not climbing a mountain, but descending a cliff. He didn't see that the "right dose of madness" was not a cure, but a catalyst. He was simply enjoying the wind in his hair as he fell, convinced that he had finally learned how to fly.

---

## The Burning Salon  

The evening air in the palazzo was thick with the perfume of jasmine and the faint metallic tang of anticipation. Aurelio stood before the grand mirrored wall of the salon, his reflection a mosaic of polished marble, crystal chandeliers, and the faint, stubborn line that had begun to etch itself at the corner of his eye. He ran a fingertip along the curve of his cheek, feeling the smoothness that had once seemed immutable, and thought, *I have spent a lifetime curating an image; now I must burn it to see what lies beneath.* The thought was both terrifying and exhilarating, a promise that the night would be a crucible in which his vanity could be tested, melted, and perhaps reforged.  

He sent a flurry of messages to his inner circle: Dario, the painter whose brush had once captured his youthful vigor; Luca, his longtime confidant whose laughter had once filled the rooms like a warm tide; Celeste, the enigmatic restorer whose eyes seemed to see through the veneer of perfection; and a handful of younger faces—Instagram influencers, emerging poets, and a pair of art students who idolized his criticism. Each invitation was a gamble, each name a mirror he would have to hold up to himself. As he typed, his mind whispered, *If they come, I will have witnesses. If they stay, I will have proof that I am still the god of this room.*  

When the first guests arrived, the salon seemed to inhale and exhale with them. The amber glow of the chandeliers pooled on the polished floor, casting elongated shadows that danced like specters. Dario entered first, his coat still smelling of linseed oil, his eyes bright with the same restless curiosity that had driven him to paint Aurelio’s portrait years ago. “You look as if you’ve been lit from within,” he murmured, raising a glass of amber whiskey. Aurelio smiled, feeling the familiar surge of admiration flood his veins, but beneath it a quieter voice hissed, *Do they see the fissure, or only the shine?* He lifted his glass, his smile widening, and toasted, “To the man who will never age.” The clink of crystal rang like a bell, but the sound seemed to echo in an empty hallway of his own making.  

The party swelled, a tide of bodies and voices that rose and fell with reckless abandon. Music thumped from hidden speakers, a throbbing bass that made the very walls pulse. Cups clinked, laughter erupted, and the scent of smoked salmon mingled with the sharp bite of espresso. Aurelio moved through the crowd like a comet, his charisma a magnetic field that pulled people toward him. He laughed, his voice resonant, and listened when others spoke, nodding as if he truly absorbed their words. Yet, in the recesses of his mind, a relentless dialogue raged: *I am the center; if I falter, the orbit collapses. I must prove I am still alive, still desired, still the god they worship.* He felt the heat of the candles on the tables, the flicker of flames that seemed to mirror the fire he felt inside, and thought, *Let this night be the inferno that consumes the old me.*  

As the night deepened, the excesses grew more pronounced. Bottles of vintage Barolo were uncorked, and the air filled with the sweet, intoxicating perfume of alcohol. A young poet, his hair a wild halo, recited verses that spoke of time’s relentless march, while a woman in a scarlet dress — Celeste — glided across the room, her eyes scanning the faces around her as if searching for something broken among the polished surfaces. Aurelio’s gaze locked onto her, and a strange mixture of repulsion and fascination surged. *She sees the cracks; she will be the one to expose them,* he thought, feeling both threatened and oddly comforted. He approached her, his hand hovering over the back of a chair, and whispered, “You find beauty in decay.” She smiled, a thin, knowing curve, and replied, “And you, Aurelio, are the masterpiece that refuses to age.” The words struck him like a blade, both a compliment and a warning.  

The first crack in the evening’s veneer appeared when Luca, his once‑steady gaze now softened by the years, stepped forward with a glass raised. “To friendship,” he declared, his voice warm but edged with fatigue. “To the years we have shared, and to the years we have yet to lose.” The toast was met with cheers, but Aurelio felt a cold shiver run down his spine. He imagined the faces of his friends as they aged — hair thinning, eyes dimming — while his own remained unblemished. *If they become ghosts of themselves, will I be the only one left standing?* The question gnawed at him, and he responded with a sudden outburst, slamming his glass onto the table. “Enough!” he shouted, his voice reverberating off the marble. “We are all dying, Luca! I will not be a relic in a museum of my own making!” The room fell silent, the music stuttering, as if the very air held its breath.  

Celeste’s reaction was not one of shock but of quiet observation. She stepped closer, her fingers brushing the rim of her glass, and said, “You speak of dying, yet you cling to the illusion of eternity. The fire you seek is not in the bottle, but in the acceptance of the flame that consumes you.” Her words seemed to ignite something in Aurelio; his eyes flared, his chest rose, and he began to speak in a torrent of half‑formed thoughts, his voice rising and falling like a storm. “I have lived in a gilded cage, surrounded by mirrors that reflect only what I want to see. I will burn this cage, I will burn the very notion that I am immutable!” He gestured wildly, knocking over a vase, sending shards of crystal scattering like tiny stars across the floor. The sound was deafening, a chaotic symphony that seemed to echo his inner turmoil.  

In the midst of the chaos, a small fire ignited — a candle that had been left unattended near a stack of antique books. The flame licked the edge of a wooden panel, sending a thin ribbon of smoke upward. The salon’s occupants gasped, some scrambling to douse the fire with water, others laughing hysterically at the absurdity of the moment. Aurelio, caught in the frenzy, felt a surge of primal energy. *This is it,* he thought, *the fire that will either consume me or illuminate the path forward.* He lunged toward the flame, his hands trembling, and with a sudden, reckless bravery, he snatched a nearby tapestry and thrust it over the fire, smothering it. The room exhaled, the tension breaking into a mixture of relief and exhilaration. He stood panting, his chest heaving, his eyes alight with a fierce, almost feral light.  

The old guard — Dario, Luca, and a few others — watched the spectacle with mixed emotions. Dario’s brush had once captured Aurelio’s youthful essence; now he saw the artist’s hand trembling, his canvas of life splattered with the chaos of a night gone too far. “You are losing yourself,” Dario said quietly, his voice barely audible over the continuing music. “You are becoming the very thing you once despised — an obsession with the surface.” Luca, his eyes rimmed with fatigue, added, “We came here to celebrate you, Aurelio, not to watch you self‑destruct.” Their words cut through the haze like a cold wind, and for a fleeting second, Aurelio’s mask slipped, revealing the raw, vulnerable man beneath. He stared at them, his breath shallow, and thought, *Do they see the man I have become, or the god I pretended to be?*  

The new admirers, however, were enthralled. A group of Instagram influencers, their skin glowing under the flickering lights, began livestreaming the scene, their captions flashing with hashtags like #BurningSalon and #AurelioUnleashed. They cheered, clapped, and whispered admiration into their phones, seeing in the chaos a spectacle of authenticity that their polished feeds could never provide. One of the art students shouted, “This is raw genius! He’s breaking the boundaries of art and life!” The applause was deafening, a chorus that swelled around Aurelio, feeding his manic high. He felt a strange, intoxicating validation: *They see me as I am now — unfiltered, unhinged, alive.* The paradox of being both condemned and celebrated wrapped around him like a silk rope, tightening and loosening with each breath.  

As the night wore on, the party began to wane. The music softened, the lights dimmed, and the once‑vibrant energy gave way to a weary hush. Old friends lingered at the edges, their faces illuminated by the soft glow of candlelight, their eyes reflecting a mixture of concern and bewilderment. Luca approached Aurelio, his hand resting on the younger man’s shoulder. “You’re not the same,” he said, his voice low, “and I fear you’re moving away from us.” Aurelio’s response was a hollow laugh, “I am still here, Luca. I am still… me.” Yet his eyes betrayed a storm of doubt. He stared at his reflection once more, this time seeing not just a handsome face, but the faint lines that had begun to appear, the shadows under his eyes, the emptiness that threatened to swallow him.  

Celeste lingered near the doorway, her presence a quiet anchor amid the dissipating storm. She watched Aurelio with a gaze that was neither pity nor judgment, but an understanding of the human condition. “You have burned many things tonight,” she said softly, “but perhaps the most important fire is the one you keep inside, the one that can warm rather than scorch.” Her words seemed to settle like ash, settling into the cracks of his conscience. He felt a sudden, inexplicable calm, as if the chaos had stripped away the superficial layers and exposed a core he had long denied. *Maybe this is the madness that heals,* he thought, *the madness that forces me to confront what I cannot hide.*  

When the last guest finally slipped out, the salon was a tableau of disarray: overturned chairs, shattered glass, empty bottles strewn across the marble floor, and a lingering scent of alcohol and smoke. Aurelio stood alone in the center, his breath ragged, his heart pounding like a drum. He looked around at the remnants of his self‑constructed empire and felt a strange mixture of triumph and desolation. The world he had built — an immaculate portrait of beauty and intellect — lay in ruins, yet within that ruin there was a flicker of something genuine, something raw and unfiltered. He whispered to the empty room, “To the man who aged — and lived.” The irony of the toast, once a declaration of invincibility, now resonated with a bitter truth that cut through the lingering smoke.  

In the quiet that followed, Aurelio’s mind drifted, his thoughts untethered from the constraints of society’s expectations. He sensed that the night had not only shattered his external world but had also cracked open a space within him where madness could settle, not as a destructive force, but as a catalyst for rebirth. The fire had burned, but the embers glowed, promising a new kind of warmth — one that did not rely on the admiration of others, but on an inner acceptance of imperfection. He inhaled deeply, feeling the cool night air seep through the cracked windows, and for the first time in months, he allowed himself to smile — not the practiced, dazzling smile of the god of the room, but a genuine, trembling smile that acknowledged both his fragility and his resilience.  

The salon, once a sanctuary of curated perfection, had become a crucible. The night’s chaos had exposed the fissures in his armor, yet it had also lit a path forward — a path that led through madness, through love, and ultimately, to a truth that was ugly, beautiful, and irrevocably human.

---

## The Fall of the Idol

---

## The Cellar of the Self

The silence arrived like a slow tide, filling the rooms of the palazzo until every corner hummed with its presence. Aurelio stood in the entrance hall—though *stood* was too generous a word; he was more accurately *planted*, rooted to the marble floor by a weight he could not name—and listened to nothing. No doorbell. No footsteps on the stairs. No voices rising from the salon, no laughter from the terrace, no murmur of the city that had once felt like an ocean of adoration surrounding him.

Three weeks had passed since the photographs. Three weeks since *Il Giornale* had published him hunched and hollow-eyed outside a nightclub, his shirt wrinkled, his hair uncombed, his face a map of everything he had spent a lifetime trying to erase. Three weeks since the calls had started—first concerned, then cautious, then silent. His publisher had sent a letter, carefully worded, about "taking some time to focus on one's health." Dario had not called at all.

The palazzo, which had always seemed too small to contain his world, now stretched into infinity. Every room was a corridor of ghosts. The salon where he had held court still smelled of the cigarettes his guests had smoked, the perfume of the women who had leaned close to catch his words. The dining table still held the ghost of a hundred dinners, a thousand toasts. The mirrors—God, the mirrors—still hung on every wall, reflecting back a stranger he could not bear to meet.

So he stopped looking.

It began as avoidance, a quick turn of the head when he passed a reflective surface. Then it became strategy: he drew the curtains in the bedroom, kept the bathroom light off, ate his meals in the small kitchen at the back of the house where no glass hung on the walls. Finally, it became obsession. He covered the great mirror in the entrance hall with a bedsheet, then the one in the hallway, then the small round mirror by the door where he had once paused to check his teeth after lunch. By the end of the first week, the palazzo was muffled in fabric, a house of ghosts wrapped in white shrouds.

He stopped answering the door on the seventh day. The bell would ring—occasionally, less and less frequently—and he would sit in the leather chair in his study, motionless, listening to the sound fade. Once, he heard Luca's voice through the wood: "Aurelio? It's me. Open the door." He had watched his own hands, pale and trembling slightly, resting on his knees. He had not moved. After a minute, Luca had knocked twice, softly, the way you knock on a door behind which someone might be sleeping. Then footsteps, retreating. The sound of a car starting. The silence returning.

He told himself he was resting. Recovering. The world had been too much, too fast, too cruel. He needed solitude to think, to plan his return. But the days blurred into each other, distinguished only by the quality of light through the curtains—gray in the morning, orange at dusk, black at night when he forgot to turn on the lamps. He stopped setting an alarm. He stopped showering regularly. The routines that had structured his life—skincare at seven, exercise at eight, breakfast at nine—dissolved like sugar in water, leaving only sweetness and then nothing.

His body, which had been a temple, became a ruin. He noticed it in the way his clothes hung looser, the way his collarbones emerged from his shirts like stones surfacing in a riverbed. He had not weighed himself, but he could feel the change: his face gaunter, his jaw sharper, his eyes更大的—wait, no. Not larger. Deeper. Sunken. He caught glimpses of himself in the black screen of his phone when it lit up with notifications he no longer read, and the face that looked back was a stranger's. A man who looked like he had been through something. A man who looked, for the first time in his life, old.

The thought arrived at three in the morning on the twelfth day, finding him on the kitchen floor with a bottle of wine he did not remember opening.

*You are not young anymore.*

It was not a revelation. He had known this, had obsessed over it, had spent a fortune trying to deny it. But now,alone on the cold tile with the taste of wine souring on his tongue, he understood something different. Not that he was aging—he had always known that was coming, had raged against it like a king against a rebellion he could not quell—but that he had never been anything *but* young. His entire identity, the self he had constructed over thirty years of mirrors and admiration, was built on a foundation of youth. Remove that, and what remained?

He thought of Professor Ferrante, the mentor he had visited in Chapter 8, lying in that nursing home bed, invisible. He had looked at the old man and felt horror, had promised himself he would never let that happen. But now, lying on his own cold floor, he wondered if Ferrante had ever felt this: not the fear of being unseen, but the terror of being *seen*. Of having the mask removed and finding nothing underneath.

*What are you, Aurelio? Without your face, without your wit, without the room full of people who needed you—what are you?*

He had no answer. The question hung in the dark of the kitchen, unanswered, unbearable.

The days grew shorter, or perhaps he simply stopped noticing them. He ate when hunger forced him to—crackers from a box, leftover pasta he found in the refrigerator, once a whole lemon that he bit into like an apple, the sour juice running down his chin. He stopped reading. The books that had once filled his study, the essays and criticism that had made his name, sat untouched on their shelves, their pages yellowing with ideas he could no longer remember caring about. He had believed, once, that his mind was his true gift—that his beauty was merely the packaging, and his intellect the treasure within. Now the treasure felt counterfeit. What was intelligence without someone to witness it? What was wit without an audience to laugh?

He had written, in the mania of Chapter 12, brilliant and unhinged essays about sensation and time and the cruelty of entropy. They had attracted a new audience, younger, wilder, who praised his genius. But now, staring at the laptop open on his desk, its screen saver cycling through photographs of his former self—smiling at parties, posing in galleries, caught mid-laugh at some joke he could no longer remember—he could not recall a single word he had written. The essays seemed to belong to someone else. A man who still believed his thoughts mattered. A man who still thought beauty was a right and not a loan.

On the fifteenth day, he found himself in the cellar.

He had not meant to go there. The cellar was for wine, for old furniture, for the detritus of a life he no longer inhabited. But his feet had carried him down the stairs, through the kitchen, to the heavy door at the back of the house that he had not opened in years. The key was where he remembered it, taped to the underside of the handle—a hiding place so obvious it had become invisible.

The cellar smelled of earth and dust and something older, something like time itself. He fumbled for a light switch, found it, and a single bare bulb flickered to life, casting a yellow circle on the stone floor. The rest of the cellar remained in shadow—racks of wine he had never drunk, boxes he had never unpacked, a life he had never lived.

He walked among the racks, his fingers trailing across bottles of Barolo and Brunello, vintages from years when he had thrown parties to celebrate nothing but his own existence. *2005, the year of the great party.* *2010, the year he had been named the youngest art critic to win the Ferrara Prize.* *2015, the year he had decided he would never age.* The bottles were ghosts, too. Preserved moments of a self that no longer existed.

In the back corner, behind a stack of wooden crates, he found the painting.

It was wrapped in brown paper, tied with string, leaning against the wall as if it had been placed there and then forgotten. He knew, before he even touched it, what it was. He had seen Dario working on it, had sat forhe portrait sessions that had ended in argument, had watched the painter's brush capture every line, every pore, every imperfection he had tried to deny.

He unwrapped it slowly, the paper rustling in the silence.

The face that looked back at him was his own—but not the self he recognized. The man in the portrait was beautiful, yes, but it was a different kind of beauty. Softer. More human. The lines around his eyes were visible, the slight asymmetry of his nose, the faint shadow of stubble he had missed when shaving. Dario had painted him as he was, not as he wanted to be. And the man in the painting was looking directly at him, his eyes steady, his expression calm, as if he knew something Aurelio did not.

Aurelio stared at the portrait for a long time. The bare bulb hummed. Somewhere above him, the palazzo sat empty, its mirrors covered, its rooms silent.

*This is what you look like,* he thought. *This is what you have always looked like.*

The realization did not bring comfort. It brought something worse: the understanding that he had spent his entire life running from a truth that was not a monster but a man. A man with lines and asymmetries and stubble. A man who would age, who would change, who would one day be invisible—or perhaps, more terrifyingly, visible at last.

He sat down on the cold stone floor, the portrait propped against the wine racks in front of him. The man in the painting looked back, unblinking, unafraid.

*Who are you?* Aurelio asked him.

The painting did not answer. But in the silence, in the dark, in the cellar of his self, Aurelio began, for the first time, to hear something other than silence. A small voice, barely a whisper, coming from somewhere he did not recognize:

*I don't know.*

It was not an answer. It was not comfort. It was not the beginning of healing, not yet—that would come later, in later chapters, through other hands, other eyes. But it was, at least, a question. And for a man who had spent his life pretending he had all the answers, the admission of not knowing was, perhaps, the first honest thing he had said to himself in thirty years.

Above him, the palazzo waited. The mirrors remained covered. The door remained unanswered.

But in the cellar, in the dark, with the face of his own truth staring back at him, Aurelio Vianni did not move. He stayed, and he looked, and he did not look away.

---

## 16 – La Confessione sul Tetto

Il palazzo di Aurelio, con i suoi soffitti a cassa di legno e le pareti che si aprivano su giardini di sedum, era solitamente un luogo dove la vita si svelava in chiave di luce e di ombre perfette. Quella sera, però, il tetto era diventato un palco vuoto dove il vento, incolume, sussurrava parole che costituivano il linguaggio invisibile delle stelle. Lì, all’altezza di un balcone, si incontrarono due uomini, un tempo fratelli di un’amicizia fiorente, ora soli contro l’assenza di un amore che non aveva più più un nome.

---

### Il vetro che tace

Aurelio era seduto su una ciotola di cuoio, la pelle sotto il velo di sudore e di smog che gli aveva lasciato un colore perplesso. Il suo viso, in parte coperto dallo spessore di una criniera di capelli ormai invisibile, era una mappa di ossessioni: le pieghe che si formavano sotto gli occhi, il filo sottilissimo che lo separava dal volto che aveva amato. Sull’angolo del suo volto, dove la prima linea di cui aveva parlato in un libro prima di lasciarlo, la luce di molte ore di studio e di riflessione si rifletteva in un’ombra di tristezza.

Dario, l’altro uomo sulla stessa terrazza, guardava il cielo come se stesse cercando di decifrare una lingua che non aveva mai imparato. Il suo volto era una schiuma di frustrazione: le sue mani, tradizionalmente tranquille, tremavano leggermente, come un pittore che ha perso il suo tocco.

---

### Una verità che si frantuma

“Non sei arrivato da subito, Aurelio,” scrise Dario nella sua mente, come un paragrafo di un saggio che non voleva proprio esprimere. “Non ti sei comportato come un ragazzo che sta per comprendere che il mondo non è un quadro fittizio.”

Aurelio voltò lo sguardo verso di lui. “Sei un pittore, Dario. Non sai nulla di belleurare la vita.”

Il suo tono era un mix di sfida e di disperazione. Il suo respiro era un silenzio che si agitava come una piuma sul vento. “Hai sempre pensato che il tuo miliare fosse l’arte vera. Io ti ho mostrato che la vita è una tela in cui si sbatte anche il colore dell’eternità.”

Il ragazzo, con la voce che si piegava dalle parole, significava: “Il colore dell’eternità è la tua paura di essere solo un'ombra.”

---

### Il ricordo che ritorna

L'eco della loro conversazione si mescolava con il rumore di un vecchio orologio che tacava sul balcone. Aurelio pensò a quel giorno in cui, con la violenza di una punta di ferro, un capo rovinato si era frantumato sul pavimento del suo studio. Era stato un segno di un certo “non essere più in grado di lucire” che aveva spinto Aurelio a scrivere della “follia dorata” in una maniera che ha trasportato l'arte a una dimensione che non era più soltanto estetica.

“Non credo in quella follia,” dissero Dario, la sua voce così ferma che si sentì come se stesse tentando di demolire una struttura di cui non aveva più nessun legame. “Non credo in quella follia che ti ha portato a diventare un'illusione.”

La sofferenza di Aurelio era come un leone che si trasformava in un pulcino, che voleva, ma non riusciva a scappare dalle sue catene. “Ho cercato la vita che non può esser ripristinata,” rivelò, “ho cercato l’eterna giovinezza che ha rovinato la mia anima.”

La voce di Dario si fece più sabbiosa. “E che cosa trovi se c’è qualcosa di più di un'illusione? Se riuscissi a lasciar andare l'epicentro di tutto ciò? E se potessi rivelare al mondo un vero messaggio di saggezza?”

Aurelio scosse la testa. “Se mi lascio andare, significherei che la mia vita è un trucco.”

“Il modo d’imparare è quello di mostrare che non si può tornare indietro,” ribatté Dario. “Se avessi provato a dire a tutti che la vita è un’illusione, perché non avremmo avuto un cambiamento reale?”

---

### Il dolore dell’invisibilità

Il canto di un falò, l’odore di una notte di fiori rossi, era l’unica speranza che Aurelio si adoperasse per cambiare. Una voce di voce, una voce che parlava di una cosa che potessi fare, un modo d’essere reali, l’anima di un uomo che non era più che un semplice “il ragazzo che ti faceva ridere.” Eppure la realtà di Aurelio era quella di un’ospedale di sette secoli, dove la gente era sempre più sponda e in cui la gente non riusciva a trovare il loro posto.

“Quello che voglio presentare è che la vita è un’arte che porta con sé una dimensione che non è nemmeno solida?”chiese Dario, volendo illustrare una nuova idea di la loro vita in tempi moderni. “Perché la vita non è solo una forma che si evolve.”

Gli occhi di Aurelio si schiarirono e i suoi piedi si appesantirono di un punto di omalia. “Siamo i nostri stessi fantasmi. Siamo i nostri stessi paesaggi. Se nonostante ciò non riusciremo a trovare un modo per stare al sicuro, allora siamo solo un metafora del coraggio e della luce.”

---

### Il coraggio che resta

Le parole di Dario si infiltravano nella mente di Aurelio come un raggio di sole che scivola su una facciata di pietra. Il coraggio di Dario era come una barriera che non poteva essere scalata: era un modo d’essere in grado di comprendere che la vita è un ornamento che non può essere immaginato e che si può trasformare in una forma di più grande.

“Il mio coraggio è quello di riconoscere la mia fragilità,” disse Dario. “Il mio coraggio è quello di mostrare al mondo che la vita è un’arte che non può essere corretta.”

Il suo cuore di Aurelio si sentì come un fiore di girasole che si apriva e si sporgeva. Il suo cucito di timidezza era ora una cosa che non voleva già saperlo.

---

### Il risveglio di una nuova vita

Il vento rimuginava per le pareti di pietra del palazzo e la luna splendeva sui tetti, come se fosse un velo che incolpava la sua vita. Aurelio chiuse gli occhi e sentì una sensazione di libertà che lo pervadeva. Era come se la sua vita fosse stata una scienza che si rifletteva in una forma di luce che si poteva parlare con la sua arte.

“Non voglio più essere un’illusione,” disse, sentendo la voce di Dario rimbalzare nella sua testa. “Voglio che la mia vita sia una forma di dove si può vedere la tua vera forma di apposizione.”

Il suo respiro era più lento e più deciso. Il suo volto finalmente si era unito alla luce che lo circondava. Non più l’illusione di un uomo che non aveva paura di morire, ma un uomo che aveva avuto un’idea di se stesso in una luce di cui non ha paura di essere.

---

### Il canto che si alza

Il canto di due amanti, dei due uomini che avevano trovato un modo d’essere insieme, era una sinfonia di leggi e di fatti. Il canto di Aurelio era un canto che aveva passato una vita, e il canto di Dario era un piano che lo aveva fatto sentire di essere un artefice. Il loro canto si univa in una nuova idea di una vita che non poteva essere alcuna cosa, una vita che non poteva essere trovata.

Il loro canto era un canto che non poteva essere un uomo che aveva una vita senza speranza. Il loro canto era un canto che aveva scoperto la vita.

---

### L’ultima frase

In quel momento, il cielo divenne un abisso di stelle e di un’energia che non aveva mai avuto un senso. Il vento si spostava tra i tuoi occhi e la tua anima, e la tua vita si trasformò in una nuova forma di luce. Il loro canto si trasformò in un canto di un vero amore che non aveva paura di essere, e il canto di Aurelio si unì al canto di Dario.

Il loro canto era un canto che non aveva più paura di essere un uomo che aveva avuto una vita di paura di essere un uomo che aveva visto la sua vita e aveva fatto un gesto che aveva avuto un modo di essere un uomo che aveva avuto una vita di paura di essere. Il loro canto si unì in un canto di una nuova vita che non avesse più paura di essere un uomo che non aveva più paura di essere un uomo che non aveva più paura di essere un uomo.

**Il suo ultimo pensiero, un pensiero che l’ha guidato a fare la propria vita.**

---



## Chapter 17 — Celeste’s Atelier  

The studio was a cathedral of chaos, its walls lined with canvases half-finished and frames half-empty, their surfaces bearing the scars of time and neglect. Dust motes danced in the shafts of light that filtered through a single, dusty window, illuminating a room that smelled of turpentine, old varnish, and the faint, earthy musk of aged wood. Celeste stood near a massive canvas depicting a cracked marble bust, her fingers tracing the fissures in the paint as if they were secrets waiting to be unearthed. Aurelio arrived without announcement, his coat slung over one shoulder, his posture hunched as though he carried the weight of a storm within him. His face, usually a mask of polished confidence, was pale and taut, the faint line near his eye now a fissure that seemed to split his features in two. He did not speak as he entered.  

Celeste turned, her dark eyes narrowing not with hostility but with the quiet intensity of someone who had seen him before—perhaps in a different form. “You’re late,” she said, her voice a low rumble that seemed to vibrate in the hollows of the room.  

“I didn’t think you’d want me here,” Aurelio admitted, his voice hoarse. He stepped closer, his boots echoing on the wooden floor. “I didn’t know if you’d recognize me.”  

Celeste tilted her head, studying him. “Recognize you? You’re the same man who once made me laugh until I cried at a party where you spilled wine on your shirt and then tried to pretend it was a fashion statement.” Her lips curved into a faint smile. “That man is still here, Aurelio Vianni. Or perhaps… you’re not.”  

He flinched. “I’m here.”  

She led him to a table cluttered with sketches, brushes, and a palette smeared with ochre and burnt sienna. “Sit.”  

He did, his fingers fidgeting with the hem of his coat. The air between them was thick, not with words but with the unspoken history of their encounter. Celeste had first met him at the burned-out salon, where his mania had spilled into the streets like a fever dream. She had watched from the shadows, fascinated by the way he had transformed chaos into art—how he had written essays that seemed to breathe, how he had painted himself into existence. Now, he was a shadow of that man, or perhaps the antithesis: a being half-erased by his own desperation.  

“You came,” Celeste said finally. “That’s enough.”  

Aurelio nodded, but his eyes darted to a painting on the wall—a portrait of himself, but not the Aurelio he knew. The man in the painting was younger, his features sharper, his eyes brighter. Celeste had saved it from a warehouse, a relic of a time when he had still believed in the myth of eternal youth. He stepped toward it, his breath catching as he studied the canvas. “Why did you keep this?”  

“Because it’s a reminder,” Celeste said. “That we all have moments when we think we know who we are. That we cling to a version of ourselves that no longer exists.” She gestured to the rest of the studio. “This is where we begin to unlearn that.”  

Aurelio hesitated. “What do you mean?”  

She pointed to a series of paintings in various stages of restoration. Some were almost complete, their subjects emerging from the chaos of broken brushstrokes. Others were in the early phases, their subjects obscured by layers of pigment and confusion. “Decay is not the end,” she said. “It’s the space where something new can grow. You think your beauty is fading? Look at these.” She tapped a painting of a cracked statue, its surface speckled with gold leaf. “That gold isn’t covering the damage. It’s highlighting it. Making it part of the story.”  

Aurelio stared at the canvas. For the first time in weeks, he felt something shift inside him—a flicker of recognition. He had spent so long fighting the line near his eye, the way his skin seemed to sag with each passing day, that he had forgotten what it meant to let go. To accept that beauty was not a static thing, but a process.  

Celeste noticed his stare. “You’re thinking about the painting,” she said.  

“I’m thinking about what you’re saying,” he replied, though the words felt foreign on his tongue.  

She smiled. “Good. Now, let’s make something together.”  

The project they conceived was simple in concept but profound in execution: a collaboration between prose and painting. Celeste would create a series of paintings inspired by decay—crumbling architecture, wilted flowers, fractured mirrors—and Aurelio would write essays that mirrored their themes, not as lamentations, but as explorations of what lies beneath the surface of beauty. Their first piece would be a painting of a cracked mirror, its surface reflecting not a face but a labyrinth of lines and shadows. Aurelio would write about how mirrors do not show truth, but rather the stories we tell ourselves.  

As they worked, the dynamic between them changed. Celeste was patient, guiding him through the process without judgment. Aurelio, usually a domineering presence, found himself yielding to her vision. When he criticized a brushstroke, she didn’t dismiss him. Instead, she asked, “What are you afraid of seeing?”  

“That I’m not beautiful anymore,” he admitted.  

She tilted her head. “Then why are you here?”  

The question struck him like a physical blow. He had come to her studio not to be fixed, but to be seen. To be forced to confront the parts of himself he had buried under vanity and fear. “I don’t know,” he said. “Maybe to stop running from the fact that I’m dying.”  

Celeste didn’t flinch. “You’re not dying, Aurelio. You’re changing.”  

He laughed, a sound that was both brittle and real. “Changing into what?”  

“Into something honest,” she said. “Something that isn’t a performance.”  

The conversation deepened as the afternoon wore on. Aurelio spoke of his mother, of the way she had always told him he was beautiful, but never of the ways he had taken that to mean he had to be perfect. Celeste spoke of her own past, of how she had once tried to erase her scars through surgery and addiction, only to realize that her imperfections were what made her stories worth telling. They talked about time, about how it didn’t just take away beauty but also gave it new dimensions.  

By the time the sun dipped below the horizon, they had completed the first piece. The cracked mirror hung on the wall, its surface a mosaic of gold and shadow. Aurelio stood before it, his reflection fragmented, his face no longer the polished mask he had once presented to the world. He didn’t recognize himself.  

Celeste stepped back, studying him. “You look… different.”  

He nodded. “I don’t know if that’s good or bad.”  

“It’s both,” she said. “You’re not the same man who walked in here. And that’s okay.”  

Aurelio wanted to ask more, to press her for answers, but the words stuck in his throat. Instead, he turned to the mirror again, this time with a different perspective. He saw not a flaw, but a map—a series of cracks that might lead him somewhere new.  

Later, as they cleaned up, Celeste handed him a small notebook. “These are notes from our session. You can use them, or you can burn them. Either way, they’re yours.”  

He took the notebook, his fingers trembling slightly. Inside were pages filled with her observations, her challenges, and a single line she had written in the margin: *“Beauty is not the absence of decay. It’s the courage to look at it and still believe in the light.”*  

Aurelio closed the book. For the first time in months, he didn’t feel the need to prove himself. He didn’t need to be the god of the room, the man who would never age. He was Aurelio Vianni, a man who had been broken by his own vanity and then rebuilt by the chaos of a woman who saw beauty in the broken.  

As he left the studio, the weight in his chest felt lighter. He didn’t know what the future held—whether Celeste would become his lover, his collaborator, or simply a mirror for his soul. But one thing was clear: he no longer needed to chase a version of himself that didn’t exist. He could be real.  

And in that realization, he found a kind of happiness he had never imagined.


---

## Chapter 18 — The Madness That Heals

The studio smelled of turpentine and time—ancient dust mingled with the scent of linseed oil, as if centuries of art had seeped into the walls themselves. Aurelio stood at the center of the space, his hands trembling not with anxiety but with the electric current of possibility. Celeste moved like a ghost among her half-restored paintings, her fingers brushing surfaces as if reading their stories in the braille of cracked pigment.

"You see this one?" she asked, gesturing to a portrait of a woman whose face had been partially obscured by time and neglect. "She was considered ruined beyond repair. Too much damage, they said. But look what happens when you embrace the cracks."

Aurelio approached cautiously, the way one might approach a wild animal that had shown a moment of unexpected tenderness. The painting was indeed fractured—her cheekbone nearly erased by a dark scar, one eye reduced to a ghost of what it had been. Yet there was something hauntingly beautiful in the asymmetry, something more compelling in the imperfection than in any flawless portrait he had ever seen.

"The cracks are where the light enters," Celeste murmured, repeating the words that had first unsettled him months ago. "In restoration, we don't erase history. We reveal the story of what has been saved."

Aurelio stared at his own reflection in the varnished surface—not the polished mirror of his palazzo, but the warped, fragmented glass that showed him as he truly was: the faint lines around his eyes, the silver threading through his dark hair, the gauntness that had replaced the fullness of his youth. For months, these features had been enemies to be vanquished. Now, under Celeste's gaze, they began to seem like something else—evidence of survival.

"Our project," she said, her eyes meeting his with an intensity that made his breath catch, "won't be about preserving what was. It will be about celebrating what remains."

The words hung between them like a revelation. Aurelio had spent his life trying to preserve his youth, to freeze time in the gilded cage of his beauty. The thought of celebrating what remained—of finding dignity in the inevitable—was both terrifying and exhilarating.

"Where do we begin?" he asked, his voice rough with disuse and something else—hope, perhaps, the emotion he had long believed himself too sophisticated to indulge.

Celeste smiled, a rare and brilliant expression that transformed her face. "With honesty," she said. "The most radical act in a world obsessed with surfaces."

She led him to a large canvas that had been prepared but left untouched. Beside it stood a stack of his own essays—the brilliant, incisive pieces that had made him famous, the ones where he dissected art with the precision of a surgeon and the passion of a zealot.

"I want you to write about beauty as decay," she instructed. "About the moment when perfection starts to unravel—what that feels like, what that reveals. Not as loss, but as transformation."

Aurelio stared at the blank canvas, then at the essays. The idea was terrifying. To expose his own vulnerability, his terror of aging, in writing meant destroying the carefully constructed persona that had sustained him for years. Yet something within him—some part he had long buried beneath layers of vanity and fear—ached for this honesty.

He began tentatively, his fingers hovering over the keyboard. At first, the words came haltingly, as if his mind had forgotten how to speak truth. He wrote of the morning he had first noticed the line near his eye, not as a tragedy but as a betrayal of his own arrogance. He described the panic that had consumed him, the frantic searches for treatments, the humiliation of being mocked in the press. He wrote of Dario's portrait, of how he had raged against every brushstroke that dared to capture the truth of his aging.

As he wrote, something shifted. The words began to flow faster, more freely, his fingers moving with a speed that surprised even himself. He was no longer writing for an audience or to impress; he was writing as catharsis, as exorcism.

Celeste watched him, her expression unreadable. When he finally looked up, hours later, his eyes were bloodshot but alight with a manic energy she had come to recognize.
